        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2068889545" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7049630031449496968">This book is every bit as wonderful as everyone has told me it would be. I'm so glad I finally got around to reading it.<br/><br/>Set in small town Minnesota in the summer of 1961, it was a simpler, more carefree time when children were free to roam on their own. Thirteen year old Frank Drum and his younger brother Jake would disappear on their bikes from dawn to dusk, to explore along the river bank and railway line or join in an impromptu ball game. However, this summer tragedy will strike at the heart </span>
  <span id="freeText7049630031449496968" style="display:none">This book is every bit as wonderful as everyone has told me it would be. I'm so glad I finally got around to reading it.<br /><br />Set in small town Minnesota in the summer of 1961, it was a simpler, more carefree time when children were free to roam on their own. Thirteen year old Frank Drum and his younger brother Jake would disappear on their bikes from dawn to dusk, to explore along the river bank and railway line or join in an impromptu ball game. However, this summer tragedy will strike at the heart of their family and change them all forever. <br /><br />Seen through Frank's eyes, the novel reminded me somewhat of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/2657.To_Kill_a_Mockingbird__To_Kill_a_Mockingbird___1_" title="To Kill a Mockingbird (To Kill a Mockingbird, #1) by Harper Lee" rel="nofollow noopener">To Kill a Mockingbird</a>, with Frank's father Nathan, a Methodist minister and advocate for fairness and justice and the close knit small town community that is protective of its young, but is all too ready to blame an itinerant native Indian as soon as a crime is committed. Frank's mother is bitter that she had to give up a career in music to become a Minister's wife (instead of a lawyer's wife) and has invested her hopes in Frank's musically talented sister Ariel who will be going to the Juillard at the end of the summer.<br /><br />This is a beautifully written novel, with meaningful and tender prose. The relationship between the two boys, their courage and the love and faith they have in their family is beautifully rendered and will stay in my memory for a long time. </span>
  <a data-text-id="7049630031449496968" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer946553604" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1485564674844315286">Probably more 3.5 stars.<br/><br/>This was a lovely book. I think many people will love it very much. I have my effervescent Goodreads friend Christine to thank for guiding me to this one, we both decided to read a book that both of us have loved, and it just happened to fit in at the same time for each of us. This was a brilliant idea, I’d recommend it. <br/><br/>My peculiar quirks do not suit all genres, I have learnt only just today. A book that is to be savoured and really pleasantly enjoyed and meandered throu</span>
  <span id="freeText1485564674844315286" style="display:none">Probably more 3.5 stars.<br /><br />This was a lovely book. I think many people will love it very much. I have my effervescent Goodreads friend Christine to thank for guiding me to this one, we both decided to read a book that both of us have loved, and it just happened to fit in at the same time for each of us. This was a brilliant idea, I’d recommend it. <br /><br />My peculiar quirks do not suit all genres, I have learnt only just today. A book that is to be savoured and really pleasantly enjoyed and meandered through and not rushed doesn’t quite suit me. I am impatient! This isn’t a bad thing, but I truly feel I do miss things. This is quite a story, a summer that should be full of innocence and outdoor play, turns into an alarmingly sad tale of murder, suicide and twisted endings. <br /><br />I have not read any books by this author, and I think I would like to read more of his work. The writing is very good, the imagery of 1960’s America was so vivid, and the storyline a beautiful piece. It’s a wonderful story of a tragic summer of loss for the family of a Methodist minister, I loved all of the characters, particularly young Jake and Frank, who were wise beyond their years. I appreciated and really felt for their mother who felt somewhat trapped in a marriage with the lovely Minister, this was a very real scenario for me. The epilogue was very satisfying.<br /><br />Liked this one, not loved – but I am sure I am in the minority here. The wow factor for me here was the title, when these two words were uttered toward the end, I had goose bumps. Loved this. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="1485564674844315286" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2904060905" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10473648177714410573">I implore all of you to read this book! It is heartbreakingly mesmerizing in it's writing, not a word is poorly chosen or out of place. William Kent Krueger writes of a young boy, Frank, and his family as they ride the waves of love, loss, accidents, death and mercy in a little town in the Midwest. <br/><br/>Frank narrates the story forty years after the occurrence of the death of his sister, and how the events leading up to, and after her death shaped the lives of his younger brother, Jake, and himself. </span>
  <span id="freeText10473648177714410573" style="display:none">I implore all of you to read this book! It is heartbreakingly mesmerizing in it's writing, not a word is poorly chosen or out of place. William Kent Krueger writes of a young boy, Frank, and his family as they ride the waves of love, loss, accidents, death and mercy in a little town in the Midwest. <br /><br />Frank narrates the story forty years after the occurrence of the death of his sister, and how the events leading up to, and after her death shaped the lives of his younger brother, Jake, and himself. <br /><br />This book explores race relations, faith (and the moments when we wish to believe, but simply are stripped of the last shred of belief in God), education, special needs, and of course GRACE.<br /><br />The voice of William Kent Krueger reminded me a bit of Wallace Stegner, and the way the sing songy lyricism flowed across the pages. <br /><br />A friend recommended that I read this book, and it's the best book I've read this year without question or reserve.<br /><br />"In your dark night, I urge you to hold to your faith, to embrace hope, and to bear your love before you like a burning candle, for I promise that it will light your way."<br /><br />If I could give this book twenty stars, I would.</span>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/637136795?book_show_action=true">Jun 09, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Florence (Lefty) MacIntosh" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Florence (Lefty) MacIntosh" href="/user/show/10856439-florence-lefty-macintosh">Florence (Lefty) MacIntosh</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Fans of small town drama and coming of age stories</span></div>

          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Florence (Lefty) by: </span>
      Most of my friends

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/10856439-florence-lefty-macintosh?shelf=mystery-crime">mystery-crime</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/10856439-florence-lefty-macintosh?shelf=ya">ya</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/10856439-florence-lefty-macintosh?shelf=2013">2013</a>,

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/10856439-florence-lefty-macintosh?shelf=religion">religion</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/10856439-florence-lefty-macintosh?shelf=dark">dark</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/10856439-florence-lefty-macintosh?shelf=reviewed">reviewed</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/10856439-florence-lefty-macintosh?shelf=rural-smalltown">rural-smalltown</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/10856439-florence-lefty-macintosh?shelf=didn-t-click">didn-t-click</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer637136795" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11417069311335888438">When the majority of your friends love a book that you didn't do you find yourself second guessing? Searching for that magic that everyone else felt and you missed? I won’t bore you by analyzing this to death <a class='jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction'>(view spoiler)</a><span class='spoilerContainer' style='display: none'>[and there’s lot of death in this book –1 murder just forgotten and left unsolved… <a class='jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction'>(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> Worth reading and a solid 3 stars, ordinary rather than extraordinary.<br/><br/>Circa 1960’s told through the eyes of 13 yr old Frank Drum a series of deaths disrupt the lives of everyone </span>
  <span id="freeText11417069311335888438" style="display:none">When the majority of your friends love a book that you didn't do you find yourself second guessing? Searching for that magic that everyone else felt and you missed? I won’t bore you by analyzing this to death <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[and there’s lot of death in this book –1 murder just forgotten and left unsolved… <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> Worth reading and a solid 3 stars, ordinary rather than extraordinary.<br /><br />Circa 1960’s told through the eyes of 13 yr old Frank Drum a series of deaths disrupt the lives of everyone in a small town in Minnesota.<br /><b>It has its strengths</b> The dynamics between Frank, a kid with a bad habit of eavesdropping and his younger brother Jake who struggles with a debilitating stutter - great. Admired his ability to capture time &amp; place. Childhood memories came flooding back – a summer day spent crossing an old trestle bridge, following a river, wandering down forbidden railroad tracks, the smell of creosote, the blasting heat relieved by an unexpected breeze – bliss.<br /><b>And its weaknesses</b> Tad slow paced &amp; predictable. While the characters are well developed most are stereotypes. The casting of the ‘non-believer’ portrayed as weak &amp; shallow, the minister’s wife –she smokes, she drinks, she’d rather play the piano than cook and clean, she feeds her children spam sandwiches!! In fairness Jake an exception - interesting &amp; well developed, wish he’d been the main protagonist. <br />Bottom-line I guess it’s focus on ‘The awful grace of God’ combined with loss of innocence brought on by death and bereavement was just a bit too gloomy for me. Probably not helped by my mood (damn these hotflashes) Many people found it uplifting.<br />If you’re looking for something with a similar flavor (even down to a Methodist minister as the father) suggest you try <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/7632310.The_Homecoming_of_Samuel_Lake" title="The Homecoming of Samuel Lake by Jenny Wingfield" rel="nofollow noopener">The Homecoming of Samuel Lake</a> Another character driven coming of age story that’s also getting a lot of buzz. It has the same high drama bordering on tragedy, but it’s lightened with gentle humor. </span>
  <a data-text-id="11417069311335888438" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1102842184" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1102842184" />
    <a title="Barbara" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2172096-barbara"><img alt="Barbara" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1598060221p2/2172096.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1102842184?book_show_action=true">Nov 10, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Barbara" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Barbara" href="/user/show/2172096-barbara">Barbara</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1102842184" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6160232938555687358"><br/>The narrator of this story is Frank Drum, a 13-year-old boy who lives in a small Minnesota town with his family: younger brother Jake, who has a severe stutter; older sister Ariel, a talented musician destined for Julliard; father Nathan, an earnest Methodist pastor; and mother Ruth, choir director and superb singer who thought she was marrying a future lawyer and is not happy with her husband's vocation. The Drums are an average family with loving parents and nice children who behave as typical</span>
  <span id="freeText6160232938555687358" style="display:none"><br>The narrator of this story is Frank Drum, a 13-year-old boy who lives in a small Minnesota town with his family: younger brother Jake, who has a severe stutter; older sister Ariel, a talented musician destined for Julliard; father Nathan, an earnest Methodist pastor; and mother Ruth, choir director and superb singer who thought she was marrying a future lawyer and is not happy with her husband's vocation. The Drums are an average family with loving parents and nice children who behave as typical children do.<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1583855686i/29085557.jpg" alt="" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>The book - structured as a mystery - is really about death and faith. As the tale opens it's the very hot summer of 1961 and the town seems to be experiencing more than it's share of death. A schoolmate of the Drum brothers, playing on the railroad tracks, is killed by a train - and soon afterwards the brothers find a dead hobo in almost the same spot. <br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1583855686i/29085558.jpg" alt="" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>This naturally get the boys thinking about death, and God, and heaven - sentiments apparently reinforced by regularly attending three church services every Sunday where their father preaches, their mother conducts the choir, and their sister plays the organ.<br><br>Soon afterwards the Drums experiences a personal tragedy that rocks their world, tests the faith of some family members, and starts to tear the family apart. Some turn to God for comfort, others reject God for allowing such a thing to happen.<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1583855686i/29085559.jpg" alt="" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>As authorities investigate the tragedy, Frank - determined to keep himself informed - snoops around, usually with Jake tagging along. Thus the brothers discover things they shouldn't know, talk too much, and add to the damage themselves. <br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1583855686i/29085560.jpg" alt="" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>The book is filled with interesting characters: Gus, a jack-of-all-trades with a drinking problem who served in the army with Nathan; <br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1583855686i/29085561.jpg" alt="" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>Ariel's boyfriend, a son of the town's wealthiest family who drives a fancy car and has snobby parents; <br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1583855686i/29085562.jpg" alt="" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>Ariel's music teacher and mentor, a blinded, emotionally damaged man who once jilted Ariel's mother; <br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1583855686i/29085563.jpg" alt="" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>A deaf, disturbed girl with a passion for gardening; <br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1583855686i/29085564.jpg" alt="" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>A crude, bigoted cop whose prejudices get in the way of his job; <br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1583855686i/29085565.jpg" alt="" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>A rough-living Indian man who appears a likely suspect; <br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1583855686i/29085566.jpg" alt="" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>A family with an abusive father; and more. <br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1583855686i/29085567.jpg" alt="" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>The mystery of the book is resolved in a believable fashion and the characters' faith is restored by a small, touching 'miracle'. Overall a good story with a satisfying mystery and a touch of faith that's not overly preachy.<br><br>You can follow my reviews at <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://reviewsbybarbsaffer.blogspot.com/">https://reviewsbybarbsaffer.blogspot....</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="6160232938555687358" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781451645859" href="/book/show/17063903-ordinary-grace">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3195534-melanie?shelf=fiction-i-own">fiction-i-own</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3195534-melanie?shelf=some-of-my-favorites">some-of-my-favorites</a>

<span id="freeTextContainer13183271871753994591">Yay! 5 stars! I was drawn into this story right away and my feelings never changed. It is a wonderful, but sad, coming of age story narrated 40 or so years after the events take place. I especially liked the character of Gus. I felt right there in the town of New Bremen, MN with everyone. Here are some some memorable lines: <br/><br/>"How could I possibly explain my silence, my complicity in his escape, things I really didn't understand myself? My heart had simply directed me in a way that my head couldn'</span>
  <span id="freeText13183271871753994591" style="display:none">Yay! 5 stars! I was drawn into this story right away and my feelings never changed. It is a wonderful, but sad, coming of age story narrated 40 or so years after the events take place. I especially liked the character of Gus. I felt right there in the town of New Bremen, MN with everyone. Here are some some memorable lines: <br /><br />"How could I possibly explain my silence, my complicity in his escape, things I really didn't understand myself? My heart had simply directed me in a way that my head couldn't wrap its thinking around and the deed once done was impossible to undo. But it weighed on me heavily. And with all that was about to occur, that guilt over my silence would finally come near to crushing me."<br /><br />"Loss, once it becomes a certainty, is like a rock you hold in your hand. It has weight and dimension and texture. It's solid and can be assessed and dealt with. You can use it to beat yourself or you can throw it away." <br /><br />"There is no such thing as a true event. We know dates and times and locations and participants but accounts of what happened depend on the perspective from which the event is viewed."<br /><br />"As I walked away he called to me and when I turned back he said, "They're never far from us, you know." "Who?" I asked. "The dead. No more'n a breath. You let that last one go and you're with them again." <br /></span>

<div id="review_611418033" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/611418033" />
    <a title="Carol" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1166663-carol"><img alt="Carol" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1277905205p2/1166663.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/611418033?book_show_action=true">May 09, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Carol" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Carol" href="/user/show/1166663-carol">Carol</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1166663-carol?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer611418033" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5990103386152457290">As I sat down to write my feelings about this book I had to ask myself "What do I want from you, the reader"? The answer: I'd like you to read this book and yet I wonder if I'm going to entice you.<br/><br/>Can an experienced, older narrator view the past with more wisdom than he might have possessed forty years earlier the summer he was thirteen? <i>Ordinary Grace</i> and another book I recently read <i>Last Summer of the Camperdowns</i> both study long ago events in childhood from the adult perspective. The latter is</span>
  <span id="freeText5990103386152457290" style="display:none">As I sat down to write my feelings about this book I had to ask myself "What do I want from you, the reader"? The answer: I'd like you to read this book and yet I wonder if I'm going to entice you.<br /><br />Can an experienced, older narrator view the past with more wisdom than he might have possessed forty years earlier the summer he was thirteen? <i>Ordinary Grace</i> and another book I recently read <i>Last Summer of the Camperdowns</i> both study long ago events in childhood from the adult perspective. The latter is very dialog driven, the former relies more on character development. Each does a fine job of taking us back in time and immersing us in a murder to solve. <br /><br />Frank, said narrator of <i>Ordinary Grace</i> takes us back to that Minnesota summer of '61 that begins with the death of a boy. Frank's father is a minister though this does not mean it's easy for Frank's family, his brother Jake, his sister, Ariel, and his mother, Ruth to embrace the grace of God without question. There are frequent bursts of anger at this God who seems to have forsaken this family. Only Nathan, Frank's father seems to hold on to his beliefs. <br /><br /><i>Ordinary Grace</i> is at it's core about faith, It is a story about family, love, loss, hate, fear and forgiveness. It is as beautiful a story as it is one of grief. <br /><br />Frank and I are the same age that summer of '61. I can easily send myself back in time and identify with his feelings and point of view. <br /><br />Krueger writes lovely passages filled with wonderful descriptions and powerful emotion. <br /><br />	<i>"I recognized the guy in the cell. Morris Engdahl. A bad sort. Black hair slicked in a ducktail and fond of black leather jackets. He was a year older than my sister who'd just graduated from high school Engdahl didn't finish school. The story I'd heard was that he was kicked out for crapping in the locker of a girl who'd turned him down for a date. he drove the coolest set of wheels I'd ever seen. A black 1932 Ford Deuce Coupe with suicide doors and a shiny chrome grill and big whitewall tires and flames painted along its sides so that fire ran the length of the car."</i><br /><br />	<i>"It seemed to me a good day to be dead and by that I meant that if the dead cared no more about the worries they'd shouldered in life and could lie back and enjoy the best of what God had created it was a day for exactly such."</i><br /><br />	<i>"They're never far from us, you know."<br />	"Who?" asked Frank<br />	"The dead. No more'n a breath. You let that last one go an you're with them again.</i><br /><br />each of these taken out of context of the whole but a sample of Krueger's style. <br /><br />No child should have to bear the dying that Frank experiences that summer of '61. No man should have to revisit it every year. <br /><br />I don't want to tell you the whole story. I want you to read this yourself. I want to do it better justice. <br /><br />I wish the story never ended. It is a solid 5 star read for me. </span>
  <a data-text-id="5990103386152457290" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1156844197" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1156844197" />
    <a title="Andrew Smith" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5885760-andrew-smith"><img alt="Andrew Smith" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1605605555p2/5885760.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1156844197?book_show_action=true">Jan 05, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Andrew Smith" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Andrew Smith" href="/user/show/5885760-andrew-smith">Andrew Smith</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Audible Audio " href="/book/show/20695879-ordinary-grace">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1156844197" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3849331091697396616">It feels like I’m the last person on Goodreads to get to this book! There are thousands of reviews on this site, so I’ll limit my comments to my views on my experience of listening to the audiobook, which was superbly read by Rich Orlow.<br/><br/>The first thing I’d say is that it started really slowly and for quite a while I really didn’t think I’d like it. In fact, I’d rate the first section of this book only 3 stars. My second observation is that by the midway point I was convinced I’d worked it all ou</span>
  <span id="freeText3849331091697396616" style="display:none">It feels like I’m the last person on Goodreads to get to this book! There are thousands of reviews on this site, so I’ll limit my comments to my views on my experience of listening to the audiobook, which was superbly read by Rich Orlow.<br /><br />The first thing I’d say is that it started really slowly and for quite a while I really didn’t think I’d like it. In fact, I’d rate the first section of this book only 3 stars. My second observation is that by the midway point I was convinced I’d worked it all out, by which time my predominant emotion was to finish it to check if I was correct! But then it changed for me. Whether it was because I became accustomed (or entranced) by the rhythm of the book I’m not sure, but no matter what the reason I became locked in to the story. I started to care about the characters too – something that had taken me ages to grow into. And the book became one I wanted to listen to just for the sheer pleasure of being absorbed into the lives of these people.<br /><br />For me the whole thing had a feel of <a href="http://www.imdb.com/title/tt0068149/" rel="nofollow noopener">The Waltons</a> - no bad thing as it’s a show I grew up with and loved, watching with my own brothers and parents. It also had a whiff of Stephen King’s <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/11574.The_Body?ac=1&amp;from_search=1" rel="nofollow noopener">The Body</a> , this being not so much down to the narrative itself but more the atmosphere created and the fact that it was told through the eyes of a teenage boy.<br /><br />The last section – well, pretty much from half way – I’d rate as 5 stars. I loved it. I found a richness and a depth that I seldom come across. There's loads here: family, class, faith, growing up - and much more. And yes, I had worked out most of it… but not all. <br /><br />It’s a good book – almost a great book. I can fully understand why so many people loved this one. And I’ll miss the family, their friends and their neighbours.<br /><br />''Night John-Boy''</span>
  <a data-text-id="3849331091697396616" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

	<tr>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('yVPNSssVmnZSZjC15jv248Cn9OwW4I9xeK71JjofUEthxdg9e1CD15Cg4of1IVpnWXd97YZCs1jpsj7GhNAilw==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('K8Q2yeH74jexgKqOh0T7Qaa6YZ8nMR0+ffNNErgCqeGDUiO+Ub77lnNGeLyUXlfFP2ronreTIRfs74byBs3bPQ==')})
  }

After months in the fetid, overcrowded jail, she learns she is sentenced to “the land beyond the seas,” Van Diemen’s Land, a penal colony in Australia. Though uncertain of what awaits, Evangeline knows one thing: the child she carries will be born on the months-long voyage to this distant land.\n\nDuring the journey on a repurposed slave ship, the Medea, Evangeline strikes up a friendship with Hazel, a girl little older than her former pupils who was sentenced to seven years transport for stealing a silver spoon. Canny where Evangeline is guileless, Hazel -- a skilled midwife and herbalist – is soon offering home remedies to both prisoners and sailors in return for a variety of favors.\n\nThough Australia has been home to Aboriginal people for more than 50,000 years, the British government in the 1840s considers its fledgling colony uninhabited and unsettled, and views the natives as an unpleasant nuisance. By the time the Medea arrives, many of them have been forcibly relocated, their land seized by white colonists. One of these relocated people is Mathinna, the orphaned daughter of the Chief of the Lowreenne tribe, who has been adopted by the new governor of Van Diemen’s Land.\n\nIn this gorgeous novel, Christina Baker Kline brilliantly recreates the beginnings of a new society in a beautiful and challenging land, telling the story of Australia from a fresh perspective, through the experiences of Evangeline, Hazel, and Mathinna. While life in Australia is punishing and often brutally unfair, it is also, for some, an opportunity: for redemption, for a new way of life, for unimagined freedom. Told in exquisite detail and incisive prose, The Exiles is a story of grace born from hardship, the unbreakable bonds of female friendships, and the unfettering of legacy.<\/span>\n  <a The book in the photograph, an eighteenth-century religious text thought to have been taken from France in the waning days of the war, is one of the most fascinating cases. Now housed in Berlin’s Zentral- und Landesbibliothek library, it appears to contain some sort of code, but researchers don’t know where it came from—or what the code means. As a graduate student in 1942, Eva was forced to flee Paris after the arrest of her father, a Polish Jew. Finding refuge in a small mountain town in the Free Zone, she begins forging identity documents for Jewish children fleeing to neutral Switzerland. But erasing people comes with a price, and along with a mysterious, handsome forger named Rémy, Eva decides she must find a way to preserve the real names of the children who are too young to remember who they really are. The records they keep in The Book of Lost Names will become even more vital when the resistance cell they work for is betrayed and Rémy disappears. An engaging and evocative novel reminiscent of The Lost Girls of Paris and The Alice Network, The Book of Lost Names is a testament to the resilience of the human spirit and the power of bravery and       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/71170.William_Kent_Krueger">About William Kent Krueger</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/71170.William_Kent_Krueger"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1407117369p3/71170.jpg);'></div>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer4868752318964515753">Raised in the Cascade Mountains of Oregon, William Kent Krueger briefly attended Stanford University—before being kicked out for radical activities. After that, he logged timber, worked construction, tried his hand at freelance journalism, and eventually ended up researching child development at the University of Minnesota. He currently makes his living as a full-time author. He’s been married for</span>
  <span id="freeText4868752318964515753" style="display:none">Raised in the Cascade Mountains of Oregon, William Kent Krueger briefly attended Stanford University—before being kicked out for radical activities. After that, he logged timber, worked construction, tried his hand at freelance journalism, and eventually ended up researching child development at the University of Minnesota. He currently makes his living as a full-time author. He’s been married for over 40 years to a marvelous woman who is an attorney. He makes his home in St. Paul, a city he dearly loves.<br /><br />Krueger writes a mystery series set in the north woods of Minnesota. His protagonist is Cork O’Connor, the former sheriff of Tamarack County and a man of mixed heritage—part Irish and part Ojibwe. His work has received a number of awards, including the Minnesota Book Award, the Loft-McKnight Fiction Award, the Anthony Award, the Barry Award, the Dilys Award, and the Friends of American Writers Prize. His last five novels were all New York Times bestsellers.<br /><br />"Ordinary Grace," his stand-alone novel published in 2013, received the Edgar Award, given by the Mystery Writers of America in recognition for the best novel published in that year. "Windigo Island," number fourteen in his Cork O’Connor series, was released in August 2014.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4868752318964515753" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
